centavo coins</a>.</li>\n</ol></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Ecuador</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="/&#603;/: \'e\' in \'dress\'">&#603;</span><span title="\'k\' in \'kind\'">k</span><span title="\'w\' in \'wind\'">w</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span><span title="\'d\' in \'dye\'">d</span><span title="/&#596;&#720;r/: \'ar\' in \'war\'">&#596;&#720;r</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Ecuador.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/89/En-us-Ecuador.ogg" title="En-us-Ecuador.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">EK</span>-w&#601;-dor</i></a>, <small>Spanish:&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">[ekwa&#712;&#240;o&#638;]</a></span>) (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_language" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a>: <i lang="qu">Ikwayur</i>), officially the <b>Republic of Ecuador</b> (<a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <i lang="es">Rep&#250;blica del Ecuador</i>,  which literally translates as "Republic of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>"; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_language" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a>: <i lang="qu">Ikwadur Ripuwlika</i>), is a country in northwestern <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, bordered by <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> on the north, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> on the east and south, and the Pacific Ocean to the west. Ecuador also includes the <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_Islands" title="Gal&#225;pagos Islands">Gal&#225;pagos Islands</a> in the Pacific, about 1,000 kilometres (620&#160;mi) west of the mainland. The capital city is <a href="/wiki/Quito" title="Quito">Quito</a> and the largest city as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>What is now Ecuador was home to a variety of <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Ecuador" title="Indigenous peoples in Ecuador">Amerindian</a> groups that were gradually incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a> during the 15th century. The territory was <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">colonized by Spain</a> during the 16th century, achieving independence in 1820 as part of <a href="/wiki/Gran_Colombia" title="Gran Colombia">Gran Colombia</a>, from which it emerged as its own sovereign state in 1830. The legacy of both empires is reflected in Ecuador\'s ethnically diverse population, with most of its 16.4 million people being <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">mestizos</a>, followed by large minorities of European, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_people_of_Ecuador" title="Indigenous people of Ecuador">Amerindian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Afro-Ecuadorian" title="Afro-Ecuadorian">African</a> descendants. <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> is the official language and is spoken by a majority of the population, though 13 Amerindian languages are also recognized, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quichua_language" title="Quichua language">Quichua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shuar_language" title="Shuar language">Shuar</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> of Ecuador is a middle-income <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Representative_democratic" title="Representative democratic">representative democratic</a> republic with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_economy" title="Developing economy">developing economy</a> that is highly dependent on commodities, namely petroleum and agricultural products. It is governed as a democratic <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> republic. One of 17 <a href="/wiki/Megadiverse_countries" title="Megadiverse countries">megadiverse countries</a> in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conservation_International_Site_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conservation_International_Site-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Ecuador hosts many <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> plants and animals, such as those of the <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_Islands" title="Gal&#225;pagos Islands">Gal&#225;pagos Islands</a>. In recognition of its unique ecological heritage, the new constitution of 2008 is the first in the world to recognize legally enforceable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rights_of_Nature" title="Rights of Nature">Rights of Nature</a>, or ecosystem rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input <span class="toctext">Immigration and emigration</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Language"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Language</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Music"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Music</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-42"><a href="#Cuisine"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Cuisine</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">9.4</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#Art"><span class="tocnumber">9.5</span> <span class="toctext">Art</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-45"><a href="#Sports"><span class="tocnumber">9.6</span> <span class="toctext">Sports</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Health"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Health</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#Sciences_and_research"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Sciences and research</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-49"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-51"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-52"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ecuador" title="History of Ecuador">History of Ecuador</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Ecuador" title="Indigenous peoples in Ecuador">Indigenous peoples in Ecuador</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Inca_era">Pre-Inca era</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tolita_mythological_figure_in_feathered_costume_IMJ_B77.0161.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tolita_mythological_figure_in_feathered_costume_IMJ_B77.0161.jpg/170px-Tolita_mythological_figure_in_feathered_costume_IMJ_B77.0161.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tolita_mythological_figure_in_feathered_costume_IMJ_B77.0161.jpg/255px-Tolita_mythological_figure_in_feathered_costume_IMJ_B77.0161.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tolita_mythological_figure_in_feathered_costume_IMJ_B77.0161.jpg/340px-Tolita_mythological_figure_in_feathered_costume_IMJ_B77.0161.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tolita_mythological_figure_in_feathered_costume_IMJ_B77.0161.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/History_of_Ecuador#La_Tolita_culture" title="History of Ecuador">Tumaco-La Tolita</a> mythological figure in feathered costume. Between 100 BC and 100 AD. Found in <a href="/wiki/Esmeraldas,_Ecuador" title="Esmeraldas, Ecuador">Esmeraldas</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Various peoples had settled in the area of the future Ecuador before the arrival of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incas" title="Incas">Incas</a>. The archeological evidence suggests that the <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Indians" title="Paleo-Indians">Paleo-Indians</a>\' first dispersal into the Americas occurred near the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a>, around 16,500&#8211;13,000 years ago. The first Indians who reached Ecuador may have journeyed by land from North and Central America or by boat down the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> coastline. Much later migrations to Ecuador may have come via the Amazon tributaries, others descended from northern <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, and others ascended from the southern part of South America through the Andes. They developed different languages while emerging as unique ethnic groups.\n</p><p>Even though their languages were unrelated, these groups developed similar groups of cultures, each based in different environments. The people of the coast developed a fishing, hunting, and gathering culture; the people of the highland Andes developed a sedentary agricultural way of life; and the people of the Amazon basin developed a nomadic hunting-and-gathering mode of existence.\n</p><p>Over time these groups began to interact and intermingle with each other so that groups of families in one area became one community or tribe, with a similar language and culture. Many civilizations arose in Ecuador, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valdivia_Culture" title="Valdivia Culture">Valdivia Culture</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machalilla_Culture" title="Machalilla Culture">Machalilla Culture</a> on the coast, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quitus" title="Quitus">Quitus</a> (near present-day Quito), and the <a href="/wiki/Ca%C3%B1ari" title="Ca&#241;ari">Ca&#241;ari</a> (near present-day <a href="/wiki/Cuenca,_Ecuador" title="Cuenca, Ecuador">Cuenca</a>). Each civilization developed its own distinctive architecture, pottery, and religious interests.\n</p><p>In the highland Andes mountains, where life was more sedentary, groups of tribes cooperated and formed villages; thus the first nations based on agricultural resources and the domestication of animals formed. Eventually, through wars and marriage alliances of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leader" title="Leader">leaders</a>, a group of nations formed confederations. One region consolidated under a confederation called the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shyris&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shyris (page does not exist)">Shyris</a>, which exercised organized trading and bartering between the different regions. Its political and military power came under the rule of the Duchicela blood-line.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inca_era">Inca era</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ecuador_ingapirca_inca_ruins.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1148" data-file-width="1720" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Ecuador_ingapirca_inca_ruins.jpg/220px-Ecuador_ingapirca_inca_ruins.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Ecuador_ingapirca_inca_ruins.jpg/330px-Ecuador_ingapirca_inca_ruins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Ecuador_ingapirca_inca_ruins.jpg/440px-Ecuador_ingapirca_inca_ruins.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ecuador_ingapirca_inca_ruins.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of <a href="/wiki/Ingapirca" title="Ingapirca">Ingapirca</a>, this site served as an outpost and provisioning of the Incan troops, but mainly it was a place of worship and veneration to the sun, the maximum Incan God, thus constituting a <a href="/wiki/Coricancha" title="Coricancha">Coricancha</a>, dedicated to the Inca ritual.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%BAmia_de_cabe%C3%A7a_-_Povo_Jivaro_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1745" data-file-width="1715" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/M%C3%BAmia_de_cabe%C3%A7a_-_Povo_Jivaro_01.jpg/170px-M%C3%BAmia_de_cabe%C3%A7a_-_Povo_Jivaro_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/M%C3%BAmia_de_cabe%C3%A7a_-_Povo_Jivaro_01.jpg/255px-M%C3%BAmia_de_cabe%C3%A7a_-_Povo_Jivaro_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/M%C3%BAmia_de_cabe%C3%A7a_-_Povo_Jivaro_01.jpg/340px-M%C3%BAmia_de_cabe%C3%A7a_-_Povo_Jivaro_01.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%BAmia_de_cabe%C3%A7a_-_Povo_Jivaro_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pre-Hispanic shrunken head of the <a href="/wiki/Shuar" title="Shuar">Shuars</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jivaroan_peoples" title="Jivaroan peoples">Jivaroan peoples</a>).</div></div></div>\n<p>When the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incas" title="Incas">Incas</a> arrived, they found that these confederations were so developed that it took the Incas two generations of rulers&#8212;<a href="/wiki/Topa_Inca_Yupanqui" title="Topa Inca Yupanqui">Topa Inca Yupanqui</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huayna_Capac" title="Huayna Capac">Huayna Capac</a>&#8212;to absorb them into the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a>. The native confederations that gave them the most problems were deported to distant areas of Peru, Bolivia, and north Argentina. Similarly, a number of loyal Inca subjects from Peru and Bolivia were brought to Ecuador to prevent rebellion. Thus, the region of highland Ecuador became part of the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a> in 1463 sharing the same language.\n</p><p>In contrast, when the Incas made incursions into coastal Ecuador and the eastern Amazon jungles of Ecuador, they found both the environment and indigenous people more hostile. Moreover, when the Incas tried to subdue them, these indigenous people withdrew to the interior and resorted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guerrilla" title="Guerrilla">guerrilla</a> tactics. As a result, Inca expansion into the Amazon basin and the Pacific coast of Ecuador was hampered. The indigenous people of the Amazon jungle and coastal Ecuador remained relatively autonomous until the Spanish soldiers and missionaries arrived in force. The Amazonian people and the <a href="/wiki/Chachi_people" title="Chachi people">Cayapas</a> of Coastal Ecuador were the only groups to resist Inca and Spanish domination, maintaining their language and culture well into the 21st century.\n</p><p>Before the arrival of the Spaniards, the Inca Empire was involved in a <a href="/wiki/Inca_Civil_War" title="Inca Civil War">civil war</a>. The untimely death of both the heir Ninan Cuchi and the Emperor Huayna Capac, from a European disease that spread into Ecuador, created a power vacuum between two factions. The northern faction headed by Atahualpa claims that Huayna Capac gave a verbal decree before his death about how the empire should be divided. He gave the territories pertaining to present-day Ecuador and northern Peru to his favorite son Atahualpa, who was to rule from Quito; and he gave the rest to <a href="/wiki/Hu%C3%A1scar" title="Hu&#225;scar">Hu&#225;scar</a>, who was to rule from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuzco" title="Cuzco">Cuzco</a>. He willed that his heart be buried in Quito, his favorite city, and the rest of his body be buried with his ancestors in Cuzco.\n</p><p>Hu&#225;scar did not recognize his father\'s will, since it did not follow Inca traditions of naming an Inca through the priests. Hu&#225;scar ordered Atahualpa to attend their father\'s burial in Cuzco and pay homage to him as the new Inca ruler. Atahualpa, with a large number of his father\'s veteran soldiers, decided to ignore Hu&#225;scar, and a civil war ensued. A number of bloody battles took place until finally Hu&#225;scar was captured. Atahualpa marched south to Cuzco and massacred the royal family associated with his brother.\n</p><p>A small band of Spaniards headed by Francisco Pizarro landed in Tumbez and marched over the Andes Mountains until they reached Cajamarca, where the new Inca Atahualpa was to hold an interview with them. Valverde, the priest, tried to convince Atahualpa that he should join the Catholic Church and declare himself a vassal of Spain. This infuriated Atahualpa so much that he threw the Bible to the ground. At this point the enraged Spaniards, with orders from Valverde, attacked and massacred unarmed escorts of the Inca and captured Atahualpa. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pizarro" title="Pizarro">Pizarro</a> promised to release Atahualpa if he made good his promise of filling a room full of gold. But, after a mock trial, the Spaniards executed Atahualpa by strangulation.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_rule">Spanish rule</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plaza_principal_de_Quito,_an%C3%B3nimo_-_siglo_XVIII_(Museo_de_la_Moneda,_Bogot%C3%A1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="686" data-file-width="926" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Plaza_principal_de_Quito%2C_an%C3%B3nimo_-_siglo_XVIII_%28Museo_de_la_Moneda%2C_Bogot%C3%A1%29.jpg/220px-Plaza_principal_de_Quito%2C_an%C3%B3nimo_-_siglo_XVIII_%28Museo_de_la_Moneda%2C_Bogot%C3%A1%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Plaza_principal_de_Quito%2C_an%C3%B3nimo_-_siglo_XVIII_%28Museo_de_la_Moneda%2C_Bogot%C3%A1%29.jpg/330px-Plaza_principal_de_Quito%2C_an%C3%B3nimo_-_siglo_XVIII_%28Museo_de_la_Moneda%2C_Bogot%C3%A1%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Plaza_principal_de_Quito%2C_an%C3%B3nimo_-_siglo_XVIII_%28Museo_de_la_Moneda%2C_Bogot%C3%A1%29.jpg/440px-Plaza_principal_de_Quito%2C_an%C3%B3nimo_-_siglo_XVIII_%28Museo_de_la_Moneda%2C_Bogot%C3%A1%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plaza_principal_de_Quito,_an%C3%B3nimo_-_siglo_XVIII_(Museo_de_la_Moneda,_Bogot%C3%A1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major square of Quito. Painting of 18th century. <a href="/wiki/Quito_School" title="Quito School">Quito Painting Colonial School</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>New infectious diseases, endemic to the Europeans, caused high fatalities among the Amerindian population during the first decades of Spanish rule, as they had no <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a>. At the same time, the natives were forced into the <i><a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">encomienda</a></i> labor system for the Spanish. In 1563, <a href="/wiki/Quito" title="Quito">Quito</a> became the seat of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Audience_of_Quito" title="Royal Audience of Quito">real audiencia</a> (administrative district) of Spain and part of the <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_Peru" title="Viceroyalty of Peru">Viceroyalty of Peru</a> and later the <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Granada" title="Viceroyalty of New Granada">Viceroyalty of New Granada</a>.\n</p><p>After nearly 300 years of Spanish rule, Quito was still a small city numbering 10,000 inhabitants. On August 10, 1809, the city\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Criollo_people" title="Criollo people">criollos</a></i> called for independence from Spain (first among the peoples of Latin America). They were led by Juan P&#237;o Mont&#250;far, Quiroga, Salinas, and Bishop Cuero y Caicedo. Quito\'s nickname, "<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luz_de_Am%C3%A9rica" title="Luz de Am&#233;rica">Luz de Am&#233;rica</a></i>" ("Light of America"), is based on its leading role in trying to secure an independent, local government. Although the new government lasted no more than two months, it had important repercussions and was an inspiration for the independence movement of the rest of Spanish America. August 10 is now celebrated as Independence Day, a <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Ecuador" title="Public holidays in Ecuador">national holiday</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ecuadorian_War_of_Independence" title="Ecuadorian War of Independence">Ecuadorian War of Independence</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jose_Antonio_de_Sucre.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="460" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Jose_Antonio_de_Sucre.JPG/170px-Jose_Antonio_de_Sucre.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Jose_Antonio_de_Sucre.JPG/255px-Jose_Antonio_de_Sucre.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Jose_Antonio_de_Sucre.JPG/340px-Jose_Antonio_de_Sucre.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jose_Antonio_de_Sucre.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Venezuelan independence leader <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Jos%C3%A9_de_Sucre" title="Antonio Jos&#233; de Sucre">Antonio Jos&#233; de Sucre</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Entrevista_de_Guayaquil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="543" data-file-width="577" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Entrevista_de_Guayaquil.jpg/170px-Entrevista_de_Guayaquil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Entrevista_de_Guayaquil.jpg/255px-Entrevista_de_Guayaquil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Entrevista_de_Guayaquil.jpg/340px-Entrevista_de_Guayaquil.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Entrevista_de_Guayaquil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "<a href="/wiki/Guayaquil_Conference" title="Guayaquil Conference">Guayaquil Conference</a>" was the meeting between the two main Hispanic South American independence leaders. In it the form of government of the nascent countries was discussed, <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_San_Mart%C3%ADn" title="Jos&#233; de San Mart&#237;n">San Mart&#237;n</a> opted for a unified South America in the form of a monarchy, while <a href="/wiki/Sim%C3%B3n_Bol%C3%ADvar" title="Sim&#243;n Bol&#237;var">Bol&#237;var</a> opted for the same but into a republic. 1843 painting.</div></div></div>\n<p>On October 9, 1820, <a href="/wiki/Guayaquil" title="Guayaquil">Guayaquil</a> became the first city in Ecuador to gain its independence from Spain. The people were very happy about the independence and celebrated, which is now Ecuador\'s independence day, officially on May 24, 1822. The rest of Ecuador gained its independence after <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Jos%C3%A9_de_Sucre" title="Antonio Jos&#233; de Sucre">Antonio Jos&#233; de Sucre</a> defeated the Spanish Royalist forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pichincha" title="Battle of Pichincha">Battle of Pichincha</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Quito" title="Quito">Quito</a>. Following the battle, Ecuador joined <a href="/wiki/Sim%C3%B3n_Bol%C3%ADvar" title="Sim&#243;n Bol&#237;var">Sim&#243;n Bol&#237;var</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Gran_Colombia" title="Republic of Gran Colombia">Republic of Gran Colombia</a>, also including modern-day <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>. In 1830 Ecuador separated from Gran Colombia and became an independent republic.\n</p><p>The 19th century was marked by instability for Ecuador with a rapid succession of rulers. The first president of Ecuador was the Venezuelan-born <a href="/wiki/Juan_Jos%C3%A9_Flores" title="Juan Jos&#233; Flores">Juan Jos&#233; Flores</a>, who was ultimately deposed, followed by several authoritarian leaders, such as <a href="/wiki/Vicente_Rocafuerte" title="Vicente Rocafuerte">Vicente Rocafuerte</a>; <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Joaqu%C3%ADn_de_Olmedo" title="Jos&#233; Joaqu&#237;n de Olmedo">Jos&#233; Joaqu&#237;n de Olmedo</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Urbina" title="Jos&#233; Mar&#237;a Urbina">Jos&#233; Mar&#237;a Urbina</a>; <a href="/wiki/Diego_Noboa" title="Diego Noboa">Diego Noboa</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Jos%C3%A9_de_Arteta" title="Pedro Jos&#233; de Arteta">Pedro Jos&#233; de Arteta</a>; <a href="/wiki/Manuel_de_Asc%C3%A1subi" title="Manuel de Asc&#225;subi">Manuel de Asc&#225;subi</a>; and Flores\'s own son, <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Flores_Jij%C3%B3n" title="Antonio Flores Jij&#243;n">Antonio Flores Jij&#243;n</a>, among others. The conservative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gabriel_Garcia_Moreno" title="Gabriel Garcia Moreno">Gabriel Garcia Moreno</a> unified the country in the 1860s with the support of the Roman Catholic Church. In the late 19th century, world demand for <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa</a> tied the economy to commodity exports and led to migrations from the highlands to the agricultural frontier on the coast.\n</p><p>Ecuador <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abolition_of_slavery_timeline" title="Abolition of slavery timeline">abolished</a> <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Latin_America" title="Slavery in Latin America">slavery</a> and freed its black slaves in 1851.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_Revolution">Liberal Revolution</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Historic_Center_of_Quito_-_World_Heritage_Site_by_UNESCO_-_Photo_437.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="506" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Historic_Center_of_Quito_-_World_Heritage_Site_by_UNESCO_-_Photo_437.jpg/220px-Historic_Center_of_Quito_-_World_Heritage_Site_by_UNESCO_-_Photo_437.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Historic_Center_of_Quito_-_World_Heritage_Site_by_UNESCO_-_Photo_437.jpg/330px-Historic_Center_of_Quito_-_World_Heritage_Site_by_UNESCO_-_Photo_437.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Historic_Center_of_Quito_-_World_Heritage_Site_by_UNESCO_-_Photo_437.jpg/440px-Historic_Center_of_Quito_-_World_Heritage_Site_by_UNESCO_-_Photo_437.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Historic_Center_of_Quito_-_World_Heritage_Site_by_UNESCO_-_Photo_437.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Antique <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dugout_(boat)" title="Dugout (boat)">dug out canoes</a> in the courtyard of the Old Military Hospital in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historic_Center_of_Quito" title="Historic Center of Quito">Historic Center of Quito</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Revolution_of_1895" title="Liberal Revolution of 1895">Liberal Revolution of 1895</a></div>\n<p>The Liberal Revolution of 1895 under <a href="/wiki/Eloy_Alfaro" title="Eloy Alfaro">Eloy Alfaro</a> reduced the power of the clergy and the conservative land owners. This liberal wing retained power until the military "Julian Revolution" of 1925. The 1930s and 1940s were marked by instability and emergence of populist politicians, such as five-time President <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Velasco_Ibarra" title="Jos&#233; Mar&#237;a Velasco Ibarra">Jos&#233; Mar&#237;a Velasco Ibarra</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_claimed_territories_since_1830">Loss of claimed territories since 1830</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Ecuadorian%E2%80%93Peruvian_territorial_dispute" title="History of the Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian territorial dispute">History of the Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian territorial dispute</a></div>\n<div aria-labelledby="The_Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian_territorial_dispute" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:25.5em;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" scope="col" style="background-color:#C3D6EF;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_Ecuadorian-Peruvian" title="Template:Campaignbox Ecuadorian-Peruvian"><abbr style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;" title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_Ecuadorian-Peruvian" title="Template talk:Campaignbox Ecuadorian-Peruvian"><abbr style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;" title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_Ecuadorian-Peruvian&amp;action=edit"><abbr style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;" title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div><div id="The_Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian_territorial_dispute" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><span style="line-height:1.6em">The Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian territorial dispute</span></div></th></tr><tr><td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">\n<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Ecuadorian%E2%80%93Peruvian_territorial_dispute" title="History of the Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian territorial dispute">History of the Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian territorial dispute</a></b></li></ul>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Gran_Colombia%E2%80%93Peru_War" title="Gran Colombia&#8211;Peru War">Gran Colombia&#8211;Peru War (1828-1829)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ecuadorian%E2%80%93Peruvian_territorial_dispute_of_1857%E2%80%9360" title="Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian territorial dispute of 1857&#8211;60">Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian territorial dispute of 1857&#8211;60</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ecuadorian%E2%80%93Peruvian_War" title="Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian War">Ecuadorian-Peruvian War (1941)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Paquisha_War" title="Paquisha War">Paquisha War (1981)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cenepa_War" title="Cenepa War">Cenepa War (1995)</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<h4><span id="President_Juan_Jos.C3.A9_Flores_de_jure_territorial_claims"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="President_Juan_Jos&#233;_Flores_de_jure_territorial_claims">President Juan Jos&#233; Flores de jure territorial claims</span></h4>\n<p>Since Ecuador\'s separation from Colombia in May 13, 1830, its first President, General <a href="/wiki/Juan_Jos%C3%A9_Flores" title="Juan Jos&#233; Flores">Juan Jos&#233; Flores</a>, laid claim to the territory that was called the <a href="/wiki/Real_Audiencia_of_Quito" title="Real Audiencia of Quito">Real Audiencia of Quito</a>, also referred to as the Presidencia of Quito. He supported his claims with Spanish Royal decrees or <i>Real Cedulas</i>, that delineated the borders of Spain\'s former overseas colonies. In the case of Ecuador, Flores-based Ecuador\'s <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> claims on the following cedulas - Real Cedula of 1563, 1739, and 1740; with modifications in the Amazon Basin and Andes Mountains that were introduced through the Treaty of Guayaquil (1829) which Peru reluctantly signed, after the overwhelmingly outnumbered Gran Colombian force led by <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Jos%C3%A9_de_Sucre" title="Antonio Jos&#233; de Sucre">Antonio Jos&#233; de Sucre</a> defeated President and General La Mar\'s Peruvian invasion force in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tarqui" title="Battle of Tarqui">Battle of Tarqui</a>. In addition, Ecuador\'s eastern border with the Portuguese colony of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> in the Amazon Basin was modified before the wars of Independence by the <a href="/wiki/First_Treaty_of_San_Ildefonso" title="First Treaty of San Ildefonso">First Treaty of San Ildefonso</a> (1777) between the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a>. Moreover, to add legitimacy to his claims, on February 16, 1840, Flores signed a treaty with Spain, whereby Flores convinced Spain to officially recognize Ecuadorian independence and its sole rights to colonial titles over Spain\'s former colonial territory known anciently to Spain as the Kingdom and Presidency of Quito.\n</p><p>Ecuador during its long and turbulent history has lost most of its contested territories to each of its more powerful neighbors, such as Colombia in 1832 and 1916, Brazil in 1904 through a series of peaceful treaties, and Peru after a short war in which the Protocol of Rio de Janeiro was signed in 1942.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Struggle_for_independence">Struggle for independence</span></h4>\n<p>During the struggle for independence, before Peru or Ecuador became independent nations, a few areas of the former Vice Royalty of New Granada - Guayaquil, Tumbez, and Ja&#233;n - declared themselves independent from Spain. A few months later, a part of the Peruvian liberation army of San Martin decided to occupy the independent cities of Tumbez and Ja&#233;n with the intention of using these towns as springboards to occupy the independent city of Guayaquil and then to liberate the rest of the Audiencia de Quito (Ecuador). It was common knowledge among the top officers of the liberation army from the south that their leader <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_San_Mart%C3%ADn" title="Jos&#233; de San Mart&#237;n">San Martin</a> wished to liberate present-day Ecuador and add it to the future republic of Peru, since it had been part of the Inca Empire before the Spaniards conquered it.\n</p><p>However, <a href="/wiki/Sim%C3%B3n_Bol%C3%ADvar" title="Sim&#243;n Bol&#237;var">Bol&#237;var</a>\'s intention was to form a new republic known as the Gran Colombia, out of the liberated Spanish territory of New Granada which consisted of Colombia, Venezuela, and Ecuador. San Martin\'s plans were thwarted when <a href="/wiki/Sim%C3%B3n_Bol%C3%ADvar" title="Sim&#243;n Bol&#237;var">Bol&#237;var</a>, with the help of Marshal Antonio Jos&#233; de Sucre and the Gran Colombian liberation force, descended from the Andes mountains and occupied Guayaquil; they also annexed the newly liberated Audiencia de Quito to the Republic of <a href="/wiki/Gran_Colombia" title="Gran Colombia">Gran Colombia</a>. This happened a few days before San Martin\'s Peruvian forces could arrive and occupy Guayaquil, with the intention of annexing Guayaquil to the rest of Audiencia of Quito (Ecuador) and to the future republic of Peru. Historic documents repeatedly stated that San Martin told Bolivar he came to Guayaquil to liberate the land of the Incas from Spain. Bolivar countered by sending a message from Guayaquil welcoming San Martin and his troops to Colombian soil.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Peruvian_occupation_of_Ja.C3.A9n.2C_Tumbes.2C_and_Guayaquil"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Peruvian_occupation_of_Ja&#233;n,_Tumbes,_and_Guayaquil">Peruvian occupation of Ja&#233;n, Tumbes, and Guayaquil</span></h4>\n<p>In the south, Ecuador had <i>de jure</i> claims to a small piece of land beside the Pacific Ocean known as <a href="/wiki/Tumbes_Region" title="Tumbes Region">Tumbes</a> which lay between the <a href="/wiki/Zarumilla_River" title="Zarumilla River">Zarumilla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tumbes_River" title="Tumbes River">Tumbes</a> rivers. In Ecuador\'s southern Andes Mountain region where the Mara&#241;on cuts across, Ecuador had <i>de jure</i> claims to an area it called <a href="/wiki/Ja%C3%A9n,_Peru" title="Ja&#233;n, Peru">Ja&#233;n de Bracamoros</a>. These areas were included as part of the territory of Gran Colombia by Bolivar in December 17, 1819, during the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Angostura" title="Congress of Angostura">Congress of Angostura</a> when the Republic of Gran Colombia was created. Tumbes declared itself independent from Spain on January 17, 1821, and Jaen de Bracamoros on June 17, 1821, without any outside help from revolutionary armies. However, that same year, 1821, Peruvian forces participating in the Trujillo revolution occupied both Jaen and Tumbes. Some Peruvian generals, without any legal titles backing them up and with Ecuador still federated with the Gran Colombia, had the desire to annex Ecuador to the Republic of Peru at the expense of the Gran Colombia, feeling that Ecuador was once part of the Inca Empire.\n</p><p>On July 28, 1821, Peruvian independence was proclaimed in Lima by the Liberator San Martin and Tumbes and Jaen which were included as part of the revolution of Trujillo by the Peruvian occupying force, had the whole region swear allegiance to the new Peruvian flag and incorporated itself into Peru, even though Peru was not completely liberated from Spain. After Peru was completely liberated from Spain by the patriot armies led by Bolivar and Antonio Jose de Sucre at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ayacucho" title="Battle of Ayacucho">Battle of Ayacucho</a> dated December 9, 1824, there was a strong desire by some Peruvians to resurrect the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a> and to include Bolivia and Ecuador. One of these Peruvian Generals was the Ecuadorian-born <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_La_Mar" title="Jos&#233; de La Mar">Jos&#233; de La Mar</a>, who became one of Peru\'s presidents after Bolivar resigned as dictator of Peru and returned to Colombia. Gran Colombia had always protested Peru for the return of Jaen and Tumbes for almost a decade, then finally Bolivar after long and futile discussion over the return of Jaen, Tumbes, and part of Mainas, declared war. President and General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_La_Mar" title="Jos&#233; de La Mar">Jos&#233; de La Mar</a>, who was born in Ecuador, believing his opportunity had come to annex the District of Ecuador to Peru, personally, with a Peruvian force, invaded and occupied Guayaquil and a few cities in the Loja region of southern Ecuador on November 28, 1828.\n</p><p>The war ended when a triumphant heavily outnumbered southern Gran Colombian army at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tarqui" title="Battle of Tarqui">Battle of Tarqui</a> dated February 27, 1829, led by <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Jos%C3%A9_de_Sucre" title="Antonio Jos&#233; de Sucre">Antonio Jos&#233; de Sucre</a>, defeated the Peruvian invasion force led by President La Mar. This defeat led to the signing of the Treaty of Guayaquil dated September 22, 1829, whereby Peru and its Congress recognized Gran Colombian rights over Tumbes, Jaen, and Maynas. Through protocolized meetings between representatives of Peru and Gran Colombia, the border was set as Tumbes river in the west and in the east the Maranon and Amazon rivers were to be followed toward Brazil as the most natural borders between them. However, what was pending was whether the new border around the Jaen region should follow the Chinchipe river or the Huancabamba river. According to the peace negotiations Peru agreed to return Guayaquil, Tumbez, and Ja&#233;n; despite this, Peru returned Guayaquil, but failed to return Tumbes and Ja&#233;n, alleging that it was not obligated to follow the agreements, since the Gran Colombia ceased to exist when it divided itself into three different nations - Ecuador, Colombia, and Venezuela.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_dissolution_of_Gran_Colombia">The dissolution of Gran Colombia</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AGHRC_(1890)_-_Carta_XI_-_Divisi%C3%B3n_pol%C3%ADtica_de_Colombia,_1824.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3452" data-file-width="4293" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/AGHRC_%281890%29_-_Carta_XI_-_Divisi%C3%B3n_pol%C3%ADtica_de_Colombia%2C_1824.jpg/220px-AGHRC_%281890%29_-_Carta_XI_-_Divisi%C3%B3n_pol%C3%ADtica_de_Colombia%2C_1824.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/AGHRC_%281890%29_-_Carta_XI_-_Divisi%C3%B3n_pol%C3%ADtica_de_Colombia%2C_1824.jpg/330px-AGHRC_%281890%29_-_Carta_XI_-_Divisi%C3%B3n_pol%C3%ADtica_de_Colombia%2C_1824.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/AGHRC_%281890%29_-_Carta_XI_-_Divisi%C3%B3n_pol%C3%ADtica_de_Colombia%2C_1824.jpg/440px-AGHRC_%281890%29_-_Carta_XI_-_Divisi%C3%B3n_pol%C3%ADtica_de_Colombia%2C_1824.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AGHRC_(1890)_-_Carta_XI_-_Divisi%C3%B3n_pol%C3%ADtica_de_Colombia,_1824.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the former <a href="/wiki/Gran_Colombia" title="Gran Colombia">Gran Colombia</a> in 1824 (named in its time as Colombia), the Gran Colombia covered all the colored region.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ecuador1832.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="758" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ecuador1832.png/220px-Ecuador1832.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ecuador1832.png/330px-Ecuador1832.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ecuador1832.png/440px-Ecuador1832.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ecuador1832.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ecuador in 1832</div></div></div>\n<p>The Central District of the Gran Colombia, known as Cundinamarca or New Granada (modern Colombia) with its capital in Bogota, did not recognize the separation of the Southern District of the Gran Colombia, with its capital in Quito, from the Gran Colombian federation on May 13, 1830. After Ecuador\'s separation, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Cauca" title="Department of Cauca">Department of Cauca</a> voluntarily decided to unite itself with Ecuador due to instability in the central government of Bogota. President Juan Jos&#233; Flores with the approval of the Ecuadorian congress annexed the Department of Cauca on December 20, 1830, since the government of Cauca had called for union with the District of the South as far back as April 1830. Moreover, the Cauca region throughout its long history had very strong economic and cultural ties with the people of Ecuador. Also, the Cauca region which included such cities as <a href="/wiki/Pasto,_Colombia" title="Pasto, Colombia">Pasto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Popay%C3%A1n" title="Popay&#225;n">Popay&#225;n</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buenaventura,_Valle_del_Cauca" title="Buenaventura, Valle del Cauca">Buenaventura</a> had always been dependent on the Presidencia or Audiencia of Quito.\n</p><p>Fruitless negotiations continued between the governments of Bogot&#225; and Quito, where the government of Bogot&#225; did not recognize the separation of Ecuador or that of Cauca from the Gran Colombia until war broke out in May 1832. In five months, New Granada defeated Ecuador due to the fact that the majority of the Ecuadorian Armed Forces were composed of rebellious angry unpaid veterans from Venezuela and Colombia that did not want to fight against their fellow countrymen. Seeing that his officers were rebelling, mutinying, and changing sides, President Flores had no option but to reluctantly make peace with New Granada. The Treaty of Pasto of 1832 was signed by which the Department of Cauca was turned over to New Granada (modern Colombia), the government of Bogot&#225; recognized Ecuador as an independent country and the border was to follow the Ley de Divisi&#243;n Territorial de la Rep&#250;blica de Colombia (Law of the Division of Territory of the Gran Colombia) passed on June 25, 1824. This law set the border at the river Carchi and the eastern border that stretched to Brazil at the Caquet&#225; river. Later, Ecuador contended that the Republic of Colombia, while reorganizing its government, unlawfully made its eastern border provisional and that Colombia extended its claims south to the Napo River because it said that the Government of Popay&#225;n extended its control all the way to the Napo River.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Struggle_for_possession_of_the_Amazon_Basin">Struggle for possession of the Amazon Basin</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:South_America_1879.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="465" decoding="async" height="363" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/South_America_1879.png/220px-South_America_1879.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/South_America_1879.png/330px-South_America_1879.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/South_America_1879.png/440px-South_America_1879.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:South_America_1879.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>South America (1879): All land claims by Peru, Ecuador, Colombia, Brazil, Argentina, Chile, and Bolivia in 1879</div></div></div>\n<p>When Ecuador seceded from the Gran Colombia, Peru decided not to follow the treaty of Guayaquil of 1829 or the protocoled agreements made. Peru contested Ecuador\'s claims with the newly discovered <i>Real Cedula</i> of 1802, by which Peru claims the King of Spain had transferred these lands from the Viceroyalty of New Granada to the Viceroyalty of Peru. During colonial times this was to halt the ever-expanding Portuguese settlements into Spanish domains, which were left vacant and in disorder after the expulsion of Jesuit missionaries from their bases along the Amazon Basin. Ecuador countered by labeling the Cedula of 1802 an ecclesiastical instrument, which had nothing to do with political borders. Peru began its de facto occupation of disputed Amazonian territories, after it signed a secret 1851 peace treaty in favor of Brazil. This treaty disregarded Spanish rights that were confirmed during colonial times by a Spanish-Portuguese treaty over the Amazon regarding territories held by illegal Portuguese settlers.\n</p><p>Peru began occupying the defenseless missionary villages in the Mainas or Maynas region which it began calling Loreto with its capital in <a href="/wiki/Iquitos" title="Iquitos">Iquitos</a>. During its negotiations with Brazil, Peru stated that based on the royal cedula of 1802, it claimed Amazonian Basin territories up to Caqueta River in the north and toward the Andes Mountain range, depriving Ecuador and Colombia of all their claims to the Amazon Basin. Colombia protested stating that its claims extended south toward the Napo and Amazon Rivers. Ecuador protested that it claimed the Amazon Basin between the Caqueta river and the Mara&#241;on-Amazon river. Peru ignored these protests and created the Department of Loreto in 1853 with its capital in Iquitos which it had recently invaded and systematically began to occupy using the river systems in all the territories claimed by both Colombia and Ecuador. Peru briefly occupied Guayaquil again in 1860, since Peru thought that Ecuador was selling some of the disputed land for development to British bond holders, but returned Guayaquil after a few months. The border dispute was then submitted to Spain for arbitration from 1880 to 1910, but to no avail.\n</p><p>In the early part of the 20th century Ecuador made an effort to peacefully define its eastern Amazonian borders with its neighbours through negotiation. On May 6, 1904, Ecuador signed the Tobar-Rio Branco Treaty recognizing Brazil\'s claims to the Amazon in recognition of Ecuador\'s claim to be an Amazonian country to counter Peru\'s earlier Treaty with Brazil back in October 23, 1851. Then after a few meetings with the Colombian government\'s representatives an agreement was reached and the Mu&#241;oz Vernaza-Suarez Treaty was signed July 15, 1916, in which Colombian rights to the Putumayo river were recognized as well as Ecuador\'s rights to the Napo river and the new border was a line that ran midpoint between those two rivers. In this way Ecuador gave up the claims it had to the Amazonian territories between the Caquet&#225; River and Napo River to Colombia, thus cutting itself off from Brazil. Later a brief war erupted between Colombia and Peru, over Peru\'s claims to the Caquet&#225; region, which ended with the Peru reluctantly signing the Salomon-Lozano Treaty on March 24, 1922. Ecuador protested this secret treaty, since Colombia gave away Ecuadorian claimed land to Peru that Ecuador had given to Colombia in 1916.\n</p><p>In July 21, 1924 the Ponce-Castro Oyanguren Protocol was signed between Ecuador and Peru where both agreed to hold direct negotiations and to resolve the dispute in an equitable manner and to submit the differing points of the dispute to the United States for arbitration. Negotiations between the Ecuadorian and Peruvian representatives began in Washington on September 30, 1935. These negotiations were long and tiresome. Both sides logically presented their cases, but no one seemed to give up their claims. Then on February 6, 1937, Ecuador presented a transactional line which Peru rejected the next day. The negotiations turned into intense arguments during the next 7 months and finally on September 29, 1937 the Peruvian representatives decided to break off the negotiations without submitting the dispute to arbitration because the direct negotiations were going nowhere.\n</p><p>Four years later in 1941, amid fast-growing tensions within disputed territories around the Zarumilla River, war broke out with Peru. Peru claimed that Ecuador\'s military presence in Peruvian-claimed territory was an invasion; Ecuador, for its part, claimed that Peru had recently invaded Ecuador around the Zarumilla River and that Peru since Ecuador\'s independence from Spain has systematically occupied Tumbez, Jaen, and most of the disputed territories in the Amazonian Basin between the Putomayo and Mara&#241;on Rivers. In July 1941, troops were mobilized in both countries. Peru had an army of 11,681 troops who faced a poorly supplied and inadequately armed Ecuadorian force of 2,300, of which only 1,300 were deployed in the southern provinces. Hostilities erupted on July 5, 1941, when Peruvian forces crossed the Zarumilla river at several locations, testing the strength and resolve of the Ecuadorian border troops. Finally, on July 23, 1941, the Peruvians launched a major invasion, crossing the Zarumilla river in force and advancing into the Ecuadorian province of <a href="/wiki/El_Oro_Province" title="El Oro Province">El Oro</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ecuador-peru-land-claims-01.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="731" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Ecuador-peru-land-claims-01.png/220px-Ecuador-peru-land-claims-01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Ecuador-peru-land-claims-01.png/330px-Ecuador-peru-land-claims-01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Ecuador-peru-land-claims-01.png/440px-Ecuador-peru-land-claims-01.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ecuador-peru-land-claims-01.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Ecuadorian Land Claims after 1916</div></div></div>\n<p>During the course of the <a href="/wiki/Ecuadorian%E2%80%93Peruvian_War" title="Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian War">Ecuadorian&#8211;Peruvian War</a>, Peru gained control over part of the disputed territory and some parts of the province of El Oro, and some parts of the <a href="/wiki/Loja_Province" title="Loja Province">province of Loja</a>, demanding that the Ecuadorian government give up its territorial claims. The Peruvian Navy blocked the port of <a href="/wiki/Guayaquil" title="Guayaquil">Guayaquil</a>, almost cutting all supplies to the Ecuadorian troops. After a few weeks of war and under pressure by the United States and several Latin American nations, all fighting came to a stop. Ecuador and Peru came to an accord formalized in the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Protocol" title="Rio Protocol">Rio Protocol</a>, signed on January 29, 1942, in favor of hemispheric unity against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_Powers" title="Axis Powers">Axis Powers</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> favouring Peru with the territory they occupied at the time the war came to an end.\n</p><p>The 1944 <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Glorious_May_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Glorious May Revolution (page does not exist)">Glorious May Revolution</a> followed a military-civilian rebellion and a subsequent civic strike which successfully removed Carlos Arroyo del R&#237;o as a dictator from Ecuador\'s government. However, a post-Second World War recession and popular unrest led to a return to populist politics and domestic military interventions in the 1960s, while foreign companies developed oil resources in the Ecuadorian Amazon. In 1972, construction of the Andean pipeline was completed. The pipeline brought oil from the east side of the Andes to the coast, making Ecuador South America\'s second largest oil exporter. The pipeline in southern Ecuador did nothing to resolve tensions between Ecuador and Peru, however.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relevo-ecu.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="272" data-file-width="557" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Relevo-ecu.JPG/220px-Relevo-ecu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Relevo-ecu.JPG/330px-Relevo-ecu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Relevo-ecu.JPG/440px-Relevo-ecu.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relevo-ecu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ecuadorian troops during the <a href="/wiki/Cenepa_War" title="Cenepa War">Cenepa War</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mirage_F.1JA.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1700" decoding="async" height="91" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Mirage_F.1JA.JPG/220px-Mirage_F.1JA.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Mirage_F.1JA.JPG/330px-Mirage_F.1JA.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Mirage_F.1JA.JPG/440px-Mirage_F.1JA.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mirage_F.1JA.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Dassault_Mirage_F1" title="Dassault Mirage F1">Mirage F.1JA</a> (FAE-806) was one aircraft involved in the claimed shooting down of two Peruvian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sukhoi_Su-22" title="Sukhoi Su-22">Sukhoi Su-22</a> on February 10, 1995.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rio_Protocol" title="Rio Protocol">Rio Protocol</a> failed to precisely resolve the border along a little river in the remote <i>Cordillera del C&#243;ndor</i> region in southern Ecuador. This caused a long-simmering dispute between Ecuador and Peru, which ultimately led to fighting between the two countries; first a border skirmish in January&#8211;February 1981 known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paquisha_Incident" title="Paquisha Incident">Paquisha Incident</a>, and ultimately full-scale warfare in January 1995 where the Ecuadorian military shot down Peruvian aircraft and helicopters and Peruvian infantry marched into southern Ecuador. Each country blamed the other for the onset of hostilities, known as the <a href="/wiki/Cenepa_War" title="Cenepa War">Cenepa War</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sixto_Dur%C3%A1n_Ball%C3%A9n" title="Sixto Dur&#225;n Ball&#233;n">Sixto Dur&#225;n Ball&#233;n</a>, the Ecuadorian president, famously declared that he would not give up a single centimeter of Ecuador. Popular sentiment in Ecuador became strongly <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalistic</a> against Peru: graffiti could be seen on the walls of Quito referring to Peru as the "<i>Cain de Latinoam&#233;rica</i>", a reference to the murder of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abel" title="Abel">Abel</a> by his brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cain" title="Cain">Cain</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Book of Genesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ecuador and Peru signed the <a href="/wiki/Brasilia_Presidential_Act" title="Brasilia Presidential Act">Brasilia Presidential Act</a> peace agreement on October 26, 1998, which ended hostilities, and effectively put an end to the Western Hemisphere\'s longest running territorial dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucdp.uu.se_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ucdp.uu.se-18">[18]</a></sup> The Guarantors of the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Protocol" title="Rio Protocol">Rio Protocol</a> (Argentina, Brazil, Chile, and the United States of America) ruled that the border of the undelineated zone was to be set at the line of the <i>Cordillera del C&#243;ndor</i>. While Ecuador had to give up its decades-old territorial claims to the eastern slopes of the Cordillera, as well as to the entire western area of Cenepa headwaters, Peru was compelled to give to Ecuador, in perpetual lease but without sovereignty, 1&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (0.39&#160;sq&#160;mi) of its territory, in the area where the Ecuadorian base of Tiwinza &#8211; focal point of the war &#8211; had been located within Peruvian soil and which the Ecuadorian Army held during the conflict. The final border demarcation came into effect on May 13, 1999 and the multi-national MOMEP (Military Observer Mission for Ecuador and Peru) troop deployment withdrew on June 17, 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucdp.uu.se_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ucdp.uu.se-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Military_governments_.281972.E2.80.9379.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_governments_(1972&#8211;79)">Military governments (1972&#8211;79)</span></h3>\n<p>In 1972, a "revolutionary and nationalist" military <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">junta</a> overthrew the government of Velasco Ibarra. The <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d\'&#233;tat">coup d\'&#233;tat</a> was led by General <a href="/wiki/Guillermo_Rodr%C3%ADguez_(politician)" title="Guillermo Rodr&#237;guez (politician)">Guillermo Rodr&#237;guez</a> and executed by navy commander Jorge Queirolo G. The new president exiled Jos&#233; Mar&#237;a Velasco to <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>. He remained in power until 1976, when he was removed by another military government. That military junta was led by Admiral <a href="/wiki/Alfredo_Poveda" title="Alfredo Poveda">Alfredo Poveda</a>, who was declared chairman of the Supreme Council. The Supreme Council included two other members: General Guillermo Dur&#225;n Arcentales and General Luis Leoro Franco. The civil society more and more insistently called for democratic elections. Colonel <a href="/wiki/Richelieu_Levoyer" title="Richelieu Levoyer">Richelieu Levoyer</a>, Government Minister, proposed and implemented a Plan to return to the constitutional system through universal elections. This plan enabled the new democratically elected president to assume the duties of the executive office.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_democracy">Return to democracy</span></h3>\n<p>Elections were held on April 29, 1979, under a new constitution. <a href="/wiki/Jaime_Rold%C3%B3s_Aguilera" title="Jaime Rold&#243;s Aguilera">Jaime Rold&#243;s Aguilera</a> was elected president, garnering over one million votes, the most in Ecuadorian history. He took office on August 10, as the first constitutionally elected president after nearly a decade of civilian and military dictatorships. In 1980, he founded the <i>Partido Pueblo, Cambio y Democracia</i> (People, Change, and Democracy Party) after withdrawing from the <i>Concentraci&#243;n de Fuerzas Populares</i> (Popular Forces Concentration) and governed until May 24, 1981, when he died along with his wife and the minister of defense, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Marco_Subia_Martinez&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Marco Subia Martinez (page does not exist)">Marco Subia Martinez</a>, when his Air Force plane crashed in heavy rain near the Peruvian border. Many people believe that he was assassinated by the CIA,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> given the multiple death threats leveled against him because of his reformist agenda, deaths in automobile crashes of two key witnesses before they could testify during the investigation, and the sometimes contradictory accounts of the incident.\n</p><p>Roldos was immediately succeeded by Vice President Osvaldo Hurtado, who was followed in 1984 by <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n_Febres_Cordero" title="Le&#243;n Febres Cordero">Le&#243;n Febres Cordero</a> from the Social Christian Party. <a href="/wiki/Rodrigo_Borja_Cevallos" title="Rodrigo Borja Cevallos">Rodrigo Borja Cevallos</a> of the Democratic Left (Izquierda Democr&#225;tica, or ID) party won the presidency in 1988, running in the runoff election against <a href="/wiki/Abdal%C3%A1_Bucaram" title="Abdal&#225; Bucaram">Abdal&#225; Bucaram</a> (brother in law of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jaime_Roldos" title="Jaime Roldos">Jaime Roldos</a> and founder of the Ecuadorian Roldosist Party). His government was committed to improving human rights protection and carried out some reforms, notably an opening of Ecuador to foreign trade. The Borja government concluded an accord leading to the disbanding of the small terrorist group, "<a href="/wiki/%C2%A1Alfaro_Vive,_Carajo!" title="&#161;Alfaro Vive, Carajo!">&#161;Alfaro Vive, Carajo!</a>" ("Alfaro Lives, Dammit!"), named after <a href="/wiki/Eloy_Alfaro" title="Eloy Alfaro">Eloy Alfaro</a>. However, continuing economic problems undermined the popularity of the ID, and opposition parties gained control of Congress in 1999.\n</p><p>The emergence of the Amerindian population as an active constituency has added to the democratic volatility of the country in recent years. The population has been motivated by government failures to deliver on promises of land reform, lower unemployment and provision of social services, and historical exploitation by the land-holding elite. Their movement, along with the continuing destabilizing efforts by both the elite and leftist movements, has led to a deterioration of the executive office. The populace and the other branches of government give the president very little political capital, as illustrated by the most recent removal of President <a href="/wiki/Lucio_Guti%C3%A9rrez" title="Lucio Guti&#233;rrez">Lucio Guti&#233;rrez</a> from office by Congress in April 2005. Vice President <a href="/wiki/Alfredo_Palacio" title="Alfredo Palacio">Alfredo Palacio</a> took his place and remained in office until the presidential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecuadorian_general_election,_2006" title="Ecuadorian general election, 2006">election of 2006</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Correa" title="Rafael Correa">Rafael Correa</a> gained the presidency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rory_Carroll,_Latin_America_correspondent_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rory_Carroll,_Latin_America_correspondent-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 2008, president Correa declared Ecuador\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_debt" title="National debt">national debt</a> illegitimate, based on the argument that it was <a href="/wiki/Odious_debt" title="Odious debt">odious debt</a> contracted by corrupt and despotic prior regimes. He announced that the country would default on over $3 billion worth of bonds; he then pledged to fight creditors in <a href="/wiki/International_court" title="International court">international courts</a> and succeeded in reducing the price of outstanding bonds by more than 60%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TRN2009Win_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-TRN2009Win-20">[20]</a></sup> He brought Ecuador into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolivarian_Alliance_for_the_Americas" title="Bolivarian Alliance for the Americas">Bolivarian Alliance for the Americas</a> in June 2009. To date, Correa\'s administration has succeeded in reducing the high levels of poverty and unemployment in Ecuador.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romero_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Romero-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Most_Popular_E-mail_Newsletter_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Most_Popular_E-mail_Newsletter-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Ecuador" title="Politics of Ecuador">Politics of Ecuador</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Correarafael15012007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="724" data-file-width="641" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Correarafael15012007.jpg/170px-Correarafael15012007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Correarafael15012007.jpg/255px-Correarafael15012007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Correarafael15012007.jpg/340px-Correarafael15012007.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Correarafael15012007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The former President Rafael Correa assumed office on January 15, 2007</div></div></div>\n<p>The Ecuadorian State consists of five branches of government: the <i>Executive Branch</i>, the <i>Legislative Branch</i>, the <i>Judicial Branch</i>, the <i>Electoral Branch</i>, and <i>Transparency and Social Control.</i>\n</p><p>Ecuador is governed by a democratically elected President, for a four-year term. The current president of Ecuador, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lenin_Moreno" title="Lenin Moreno">Lenin Moreno</a>, exercises his power from the presidential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palacio_de_Carondelet" title="Palacio de Carondelet">Palacio de Carondelet</a> in Quito. The <a href="/wiki/2008_Constitution_of_Ecuador" title="2008 Constitution of Ecuador">current constitution</a> was written by the <a href="/wiki/Ecuadorian_Constituent_Assembly" title="Ecuadorian Constituent Assembly">Ecuadorian Constituent Assembly</a> elected in 2007, and was approved by referendum in 2008. Since 1936, voting is compulsory for all literate persons aged 18&#8211;65, optional for all other citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The executive branch includes 23 ministries. Provincial governors and councilors (mayors, aldermen, and parish boards) are directly elected. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Ecuador" title="National Assembly of Ecuador">National Assembly of Ecuador</a> meets throughout the year except for recesses in July and December. There are thirteen permanent committees. Members of the National Court of Justice are appointed by the National Judicial Council for nine-year terms.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Executive_branch">Executive branch</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_heads_of_state_of_Ecuador" title="List of heads of state of Ecuador">List of heads of state of Ecuador</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quito_PalaciodeGobierno.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Quito_PalaciodeGobierno.JPG/220px-Quito_PalaciodeGobierno.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Quito_PalaciodeGobierno.JPG/330px-Quito_PalaciodeGobierno.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Quito_PalaciodeGobierno.JPG/440px-Quito_PalaciodeGobierno.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quito_PalaciodeGobierno.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palacio_de_Carondelet" title="Palacio de Carondelet">Palacio de Carondelet</a>, the executive branch of the Ecuadorian Government</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Executive_(government)" title="Executive (government)">executive branch</a> is led by the president, an office currently held by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lenin_Moreno" title="Lenin Moreno">Lenin Moreno</a>. He is accompanied by the vice-president, currently <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Glas" title="Jorge Glas">Jorge Glas</a>, elected for four years (with the ability to be re-elected only once). As head of state and chief government official, he is responsible for public administration including the appointing of national coordinators, ministers, ministers of State and public servants. The executive branch defines foreign policy, appoints the Chancellor of the Republic, as well as ambassadors and consuls, being the ultimate authority over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_of_Ecuador" title="Military of Ecuador">Armed Forces of Ecuador</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Police_of_Ecuador" title="National Police of Ecuador">National Police of Ecuador</a>, and appointing authorities. The acting president\'s wife receives the title of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Lady_of_Ecuador" title="First Lady of Ecuador">First Lady of Ecuador</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislative_branch">Legislative branch</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Ecuador)" title="National Assembly (Ecuador)">National Assembly (Ecuador)</a></div>\n<p>The legislative branch is embodied by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Ecuador" title="National Assembly of Ecuador">National Assembly</a>, which is headquartered in the city of <a href="/wiki/Quito" title="Quito">Quito</a> in the Legislative Palace, and consists of 137 assemblymen, divided into ten committees and elected for a four-year term. Fifteen national constituency elected assembly, two Assembly members elected from each province and one for every 100,000 inhabitants or fraction exceeding 150,000, according to the latest national population census. In addition, statute determines the election of assembly of regions, and metropolitan districts.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_branch">Judicial branch</span></h3>\n<p>Ecuador\'s judiciary has as its main body the Judicial Council, and also includes the National Court of Justice, provincial courts, and lower courts. Legal representation is made by the Judicial Council.\nThe National Court of Justice is composed of 21 judges elected for a term of nine years. Judges are renewed by thirds every three years pursuant to the Judicial Code. These are elected by the Judicial Council on the basis of opposition proceedings and merits.\nThe justice system is buttressed by the independent offices of public prosecutor and the public defender. Auxiliary organs are as follows: <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_notary" title="Civil law notary">notaries</a>, court auctioneers, and court receivers. Also there is a special legal regime for Amerindians.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electoral_branch">Electoral branch</span></h3>\n<p>The electoral system functions by authorities which enter only every four years or when <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_Ecuador" title="Elections in Ecuador">elections or referendums</a> occur. Its main functions are to organize, control elections, and punish the infringement of electoral rules. Its main body is the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=National_Electoral_Council_(Ecuador)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="National Electoral Council (Ecuador) (page does not exist)">National Electoral Council</a>, which is based in the city of Quito, and consists of seven members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_parties_in_Ecuador" title="Political parties in Ecuador">political parties</a> most voted, enjoying complete financial and administrative autonomy. This body, along with the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Electoral_Tribunal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Electoral Tribunal (page does not exist)">electoral court</a>, forms the <i>Electoral Branch</i> which is one of Ecuador\'s five <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">branches of government</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transparency_and_social_control_branch">Transparency and social control branch</span></h3>\n<p>The Transparency and Social Control consists of the Council of Citizen Participation and Social Control, an <a href="/wiki/Ombudsman" title="Ombudsman">ombudsman</a>, the Comptroller General of the State, and the superintendents. Branch members hold office for five years. This branch is responsible for promoting transparency and control plans publicly, as well as plans to design mechanisms to combat corruption, as also designate certain authorities, and be the regulatory mechanism of accountability in the country.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Human_Rights_Council" title="United Nations Human Rights Council">UN\'s Human Rights Council</a>\'s (HRC) Universal Periodic Review (UPR) has treated the restrictions on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_of_expression" title="Freedom of expression">freedom of expression</a> and efforts to control <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NGO" title="NGO">NGOs</a> and recommended that Ecuador should stop the criminal sanctions for the expression of opinions, and delay in implementing judicial reforms. Ecuador rejected the recommendation on decriminalization of libel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrw_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-hrw-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a> (HRW) President Correa has intimidated journalists and subjected them to "public denunciation and retaliatory litigation". The sentences to journalists have been years of imprisonment and millions of dollars of compensation, even though defendants have been pardoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrw_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-hrw-27">[27]</a></sup> Correa has stated he was only seeking a retraction for slanderous statements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to HRW, Correa\'s government has weakened the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_of_press" title="Freedom of press">freedom of press</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judicial_independence" title="Judicial independence">independence of the judicial system</a>. In Ecuador\'s current judicial system, judges are selected in a contest of merits, rather than government appointments. However, the process of selection has been criticized as biased and subjective. In particular, the final interview is said to be given "excessive weighing."
